		\end{definition}
		\begin{definition}\label{sheaf_of_modules_c_den}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
%Definition. 
Let $A$ be a ring and let $M$ be an $A$--module. We define the sheaf 
{\it associated} to $M$ on $\mathrm {Spec}~ A$, denoted by $\widetilde M$, as follows. For each prime 
ideal $\mathfrak p \subset A$, let $M_{\mathfrak p}$ be the localization of $M$ at $\mathfrak p$. For any open set 
$\sU \subset \mathrm {Spec} A$ we define the group $\widetilde M\left(\sU \right)$  to be the set of functions $s ; \sU \to \coprod_{\mathfrak p \in \sU}M_{\mathfrak p}$ such that for each $\mathfrak p \in \sU$, $s\left(\mathfrak p \right) \in M_{\mathfrak p}$ and such that $s$ is locally 
a fraction $m/f$ with $m \in M$ and $f \in A$. To be precise, we require that for 
each $\mathfrak p \in \sU$, there is a neighborhood $\sV$ of $\mathfrak p$ in $\sU$, and there are elements $m \in M$ and $f \in A$, such that for each $\mathfrak q \in \sV$, and $s\left( \mathfrak q\right) = m/f \in M_{\mathfrak q}$. 
We make $\widetilde M$ into a sheaf by using the obvious restriction maps. 
		\end{definition}
		
		\begin{definition}\label{ag_coherent_defn}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
 Let $\left(\sX, \mathcal O_\sX \right)$ be a scheme. A sheaf of $\mathcal O_\sX$ -modules $\mathscr F$ is {\it quasi-coherent} if $\sX$ can be covered by open affine subsets $\sU_\iota = \mathrm {Spec}~ A_\iota$
that for each $\iota$ there is an $A_\iota$-module $M_\iota$ with $\mathscr F|_{\sU_\iota} \cong M_\iota$. We say that 
 $\mathscr F$ is {\it coherent} if furthermore each $M_\iota$ can be taken to be a finitely generated $M_\iota$-module. 		
 \end{definition}

\subsection{Cohomology of sheaves}
\paragraph{} Algebraic geometry operates with Zariski topology (cf. \cite{hartshorne:ag}) However some modifications of the algebraic geometry cab be applied to the considered hare spaces.
\begin{empt}\label{loc_const_triv_empt}
	It is proven in \cite{milne:lec} that if $\sX_{\mathfrak{Zariski}}$  is an algebraic manifold with Zariski topology and $\mathscr F$ is a  locally constant sheaf on $\sX_{\mathfrak{Zariski}}$  then
	$$
	\forall r > 0 \quad H^r\left(\sX_{\mathfrak{Zariski}}, \mathscr F \right) = 0.
	$$
\end{empt}
\begin{theorem}\label{quasi_zero_thm}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
%Theorem 3.5.
 Let $\sX \bydef \mathrm{Spec} A$ be the spectrum of a Noetherian ring $A$. Then 
for all quasi-coherent sheaves $\mathscr F$ on $\sX$, and for all $p > 0$, we have $H^p\left( \sX\right)= 0$. 
\end{theorem}
\begin{empt}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
	Let A be a Noetherian ring, let $S \bydef A\left[x_0, ..., x_n\right]$, and let $\sX \bydef \mathrm{Rroj} S$
	be the projective space $\mathbb P^r_A$ over $A$. Let $\mathscr O_\sX\left( 1\right)$  be the twisting sheaf of Serre 
	(cf. \cite{hartshorne:ag}). For any sheaf of ($\mathscr O_\sX$ -modules $\mathscr F$, we denote by $\Ga_*\left(\mathscr F \right)$  the graded $S$-module 
	$$
	\bigoplus_{n \in \Z} \Ga\left(\sX, \mathscr F\left( n\right). \right) 
	$$
\end{empt}
\begin{theorem}\label{projective_ag_coh_thm}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
	%Theorem 5.1.
	Let $A$ be a Noetherian ring, and let $\sX \bydef \mathbb P^r_A$, with $r \ge 1$. Then: 
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item[(a)]  the natural map $S \to \Ga_*\left(\mathscr O_\sX \right)  = \sum_{n \in \Z} H^0\left(\sX,\mathscr O_\sX\left( n\right)   \right)$  is an isomorphism of graded $S$-modules; 
		\item[(b)] $H^i\left( \mathscr O_\sX\left( n\right)\right) = 0$ for $0 < i < r$ and all $n \in \Z$;
		\item[(c)]	$H^i\left( \mathscr O_\sX\left(-r-1\right)\right)= A$.
		\item[(d)]	The natural map 
		$$
		H^0\left(\sX,O_\sX\left(n\right)  \right) \times 	H^r\left(\sX,O_\sX\left(-n-r-1\right)  \right)\to H^r\left(\sX,O_\sX\left(-r-1\right)  \right)\cong A
		$$
		is a perfect pairing of finitely generated free $A$-modules, for each $n \in \Z$. 
		
	\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Intersections and Chow ring}\label{intersec_sec}.

\subsubsection{Divisors and linear equivalence}
\paragraph{} 
Here I follow to \cite{fulton:intersection,fulton:intro_intersection,hartshorne:ag}
\begin{definition}\label{prime_divisor_dern}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
%	Definition. 
	Let $\sX$ satisfy (*). A {\it prime divisor} on $\sX$ is a closed integral subscheme $\sY$ of codimension one. A {\it Weil divisor} is an element of the free Abelian group $\mathrm{Div}\sX$ generated by the prime divisors. We write a divisor as $D =\sum_{j=1}^n n_j \sY_j $  where the $\sY_j$ are prime divisors, the $n_j$ are integers, and 
	only finitely many $n_j$ are different from zero. If all the $n_j \ge 0$, we say 
	that $D$ is {\it effective}. 
\end{definition}

If $\sY$ is a prime divisor on $\sX$, let $\eta \in \sY$ be its generic point. Then the 
local ring $\mathcal O_{\eta, \sX}$ is a discrete valuation ring with quotient field $K$, the 
function field of $\sX$. We call the corresponding discrete valuation $v_\sY$ 
the valuation of $\sY$. Note that since $\sX$ is separated, $\sY$ is uniquely determined by its valuation $v_\sY$ {\it valuation of}  $\sY$. Note that since $\sX$ is separated, $\sY$ is uniquely determined by its valuation. Now let $f \in K^*$ be any nonzero rational 
function on $\sX$. Then $v_\sY\left(f \right)$  is an integer. If it is positive, we say  $f$ has 
a {\it zero} along $\sY$, of that order; if it is negative, we say $f$ has a {\it pole} along $\sY$, of order $-v_\sY\left( f\right)$. 

\begin{lemma}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
%Lemma 6.1. 
Let $\sX$ satisfy (*), and let $f\in K^*$ be a nonzero function on X. 
Then $v_\sY \left( f\right)= 0$ for all except finitely many prime divisors $\sY$,
\end{lemma}
\begin{definition}\label{principal_div_defn}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
Let $\sX$ satisfy (*) and let  $f \in K^*$. We define the {\it divisor} of $f$. 
denoted $\left( f\right)$ , by 
\bean
\left( f\right)\bydef \sum v_\sY \left( f\right) \cdot \sY.
\eean
where the sum is taken over all prime divisors of $\sX$. By the lemma, this 
is a finite sum, hence it is a divisor. Any divisor which is equal to the 
divisor of a function is called a {\it principal} divisor. 
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}\label{linearly_div_defn}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
Let $\sX$ satisfy (*). Two divisors $D$ and $D'$ are said to be {\it linearly 
equivalent}, written $D \sim D'$ if $D- D'$ is a principal divisor. The group $\mathrm {Div}~\sX$ of all divisors divided by the subgroup of principal divisors is 
called the {\it divisor class group} of $\sX$, and is denoted by  $\mathrm {Cl}~\sX$.
\end{definition}
\begin{empt}\label{principal_div_empt}
Although the preceding definition is usually simplest to work with, it may be 
shown to be equivalent to the following more classical one. Two $k$-cycles are 
rationally equivalent if they differ by a sum 
\be\label{projective_sum_eqn}
\sum n_\iota \left(\left[\sV_\iota\left(0 \right) \right]- \left[\sV_\iota\left(\infty \right) \right] \right) 
\ee 
with $n_\iota$, integers, $\sV_\iota$ subvarieties of $\sX \times \C P^1$ whose projections to $\C P^1$ are dominant, 
and $\sV_\iota\left(0 \right)$ and $\sV_\iota\left(\infty \right)$ are the scheme-theoretic fibres of $\sV$, over $0$ and $\infty$, regarded 
as subschemes of $\sX \cong \sX\left(0 \right)$  and $\sX \cong \sX\left(\infty \right)$ 
\end{empt}

\subsubsection{Cycles and rational equivalence}
\begin{empt}\label{ag_cycles_empt}
Let $\sX$ be any variety over field  $k$. A {\it cycle of codimension} $r$ on $\sX$ is an element 
of the free Abelian group generated by the closed irreducible subvarieties of $\sX$ 
of codimension $r$. So we write a cycle as $\sY = \sum n_j \sY_j$ where the $\sY_j$ are subvarieties, and $n_j \in \Z$. 
	Let $f: \sX \to \sX'$ be a morphism of varieties, and let $\sY$ be a subvariety of $\sX$. 
	If $\dim f\left(\sY \right) < \dim \sY$, we set $f_*\left( \sY\right)= 0$ . If $\dim f\left(\sY \right) = \dim \sY$, then the 
	function field $K\left(\sY \right)$  is a finite extension field of $K\left(f\left( \sY \right) \right)$, and we set 
	$$
f\left( \sY\right)\bydef \left[K\left(\sY \right):K\left(f\left( \sY\right)  \right)\right]\cdot \overline{f\left(\sY \right) } 
	$$
	Extending by linearity defines a homomorphism  $f_*$ of the group of cycles on $\sX$
	to the group of cycles on $\sX'$.	  
			Now we come to the definition of rational equivalence For any subvariety 
			$\sV$ of $\sX$, let $f: \widetilde \sV\to \sV$ be the normalization of $\sV$. Then $\sV$ satisfies the condition (*), so we can talk about Weil divisors and linear equivalence 
			on $\sV$. Whenever $D$ and $D'$ are linearly equivalent Weil divisors on V, we say 
			that $f_*D$ and $f_*D'$ are {\it rationally equivalent} as cycles on $\sX$. Then we define 
			rational equivalence of cycles on X in general by dividing out by the group 
			generated by all such $f_*D'\sim f_*D'$ for all subvarieties $\sV$, and all linearly 
			equivalent Weil divisors $D, D'$ on $\sV$. In particular, if $\sX$  itself is normal, then 
			rational equivalence for cycles of codimension 1 coincides with linear 
			equivalence of Weil divisors. 
			For each $r$ we let $A^r\left(\sX \right)$  be the group of cycles of codimension $r$ on $\sX$ 
			modulo rational equivalence. We denote by $A\left(\sX\right)= \bigoplus_{r= 0}^n A^r\left( \sX\right)$  the graded group  where $n = \dim \sX$. Note that $A^0\left( \sX\right)= \Z$ , and that $A^r\left(\sX \right)= 0$  for $r > \dim \sX$. Note also that if $\sX$ is complete there is a natural group homomorphism, the {\it degree}, from $A^n\left( \sX\right)$  to $\Z$, defined by $\deg \left(\sum n_j P_j \right) \bydef \sum n_j$ , where 
			the $P_j$ are points. 
\end{empt}
\subsubsection{Intersection theory}\label{intersed_ald_sec}
An intersection theory on an explained in \cite{hartshorne:ag} class of varieties $\mathfrak{B}$ consists of giving a 
pairing $A^r\left(\sX \right)\times A^s\left(\sX \right)\to A^{r + s}\left(\sX \right)$  for each $r,s$, and for each $\sX \in \mathfrak{B}$, satisfying 
	the axioms listed below. If $\sY \in A^r\left(\sX \right)$ and $\sZ \in A^s\left(\sX \right)$ we denote the intersection cycle class by $\sY.\sZ$. 
	Before stating the axioms, for any morphism  $f : \sX \to \sX'$ of varieties in $\mathfrak{B}$ 
	we assume that $\sX \times \sX'$ is also in $\mathfrak{B}$, and we define a homomorphism 
	$f^*: \left(\sX' \right) \to \left(\sX \right)$ as follows. For a subvariety $\sY'\subset \sY$ we define 
	\bean
	f^*\left(\sY' \right)\bydef p_{1*} \left( \Ga_f. p^{-1}_2\left( \sY'\right) \right) 
	\eean
	where $p_1$ and $p_2$ are the projections of $\sX \times \sX'$ to $\sX$ and $\sX'$, and $\Ga_f$ is the 
	graph of $f$, considered as a cycle on $\sX \times \sX'$. 
	This data is now subject to the following requirements. 
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item[A1.] The intersection pairing makes $A\left( \sX\right)$  into a commutative associative 
		graded ring with identity, for every $\sX \in \mathfrak{B}$. It is called the {\it Chow ring} of $\sX$. 
		\item[A2.] For any morphism $f : \sX \to \sX '$ of varieties in $\mathfrak{B} ~,~ f^*: A(\sX')\to A(\sX)$ is a
			ring homomorphism. If $f : \sX' \to \sX''$ is another morphism, then $g^* \circ f^* = \left(g \circ f \right)^*$. 
		\item[A3.] For any proper morphism $f : \sX \to \sX '$ of varieties in $\mathfrak{B}$, $f : \sX \to \sX '$ is a homomorphism of graded groups (which shifts degrees). If 
			$g : \sX' \to \sX ''$ is another morphism, then  $g_* \circ f_* = \left(g \circ f \right)_*$. 
		\item[A4.] {\it Projection formula}. If $f : \sX \to \sX '$ is a proper morphism, if $x \in  A\left( \sX\right)$  
			and $y \in  A\left( \sX'\right)$, then 
			\bean
			f_*\left(x. f^*y \right) = f_*\left( x\right).y
			\eean 
		\item[A5.] {\it Reduction to the diagonal}. If $\sY$ and $\sZ$ are cycles on $\sX$, and if $\Dl \sX \to \sX \times \sX$ is the diagonal morphism, then 
		$$
		\sY.\sZ = \Dl^*\left(\sY \times \sZ \right)/ 
		$$
			\item[A6.] {\it Local nature}. If $\sY$ and $\sZ$ are subvarieties of $\sX$ which {\it intersect properly} 
			(meaning that every irreducible component of $\sY \cap \sZ$ has codimension equal 
			to $\codim \sY + \codim \sZ$), then we can write
			\bean
			\sY.\sZ = \sum i\left( \sY, \sZ; \mathcal W_\iota \right) \mathcal W_\iota
			\eean  
				where the sum runs over the irreducible components $\mathcal W_\iota$ of $\sY$ and $\sZ$, and where 
			the integer $i\left( \sY, \sZ; \mathcal W_\iota \right)$ depends only on a neighborhood of the generic point 
			of $\mathcal W_\iota$ on $\sX$. We call $i\left( \sY, \sZ; \mathcal W_\iota \right)$ the {\it local intersection multiplicity} of $\sY$ and $\sZ$
				along $\mathcal W_\iota$. 
				\item[A7.] {\it Normalization}. If $\sY$ is a subvariety of $\sY$, and $\sZ$ is an effective Cartier 
				divisor meeting $\sY$ properly, then $\sY.\sZ$ is just the cycle associated to the Cartier 
				divisor $\sY \cap \sZ$ on $\sY$, which is defined by restricting the local equation of 
				$\sZ$ to $\sY$ (This implies in particular that transversal intersections of non singular subvarieties have multiplicity 1.) 
				
	\end{enumerate}
	\begin{theorem}
 		%Theorem 1.1. 
 		Let $\mathfrak{B}$ be the class of nonsingular quasi-projective varieties over 
a fixed algebraically closed field $k$. Then there is a unique intersection 
theory for cycles modulo rational equivalence on the varieties $\sX \in \mathfrak{B}$ which 
satisfies the axioms A1-A7 above. 
	\end{theorem}
	
\section{Projective geometry}
\paragraph{}Here I follow to \cite{berger:geometryI,pjective_reometty}.
\begin{defn}\cite{berger:geometryI}
	Let $\sV, ~\sV'$ be vector spaces over $K, K'$. a map $f: \sV \to \sV'$ is called {\it semilinear} if there exists an automorphism $\sigma :K \to K'$ such that
	\bean
	f\left(\la x + \mu y \right) = \sigma\left(\la \right) f\left( x\right) + \sigma\left(\mu \right) f\left( y\right) 
	\eean
	for all $x, y\in \sV$, $\la, \mu \in K$. If $\sX, \sX'$ are affine spaces over $K, K'$ a map $f: \sX \to \sX'$ is called {\it semiaffine} if there exists $a \in \sX$ such that $\overline f: \sX_a \to \sX_{f\left(a \right) }$ is semilinear.
	
\end{defn}
\begin{theorem}\cite{berger:geometryI}
	Let $\sX$, $\sX'$ be affine spaces of same finite dimension $d \ge 2$. Let $f: \sX \to \sX'$ be a bijection which takes any three collinear points $a, b, c \in \sX'$ into  collinear points $f(a), f(b), f(c)\in \sX'$. Then $f$ is semiaffine
\end{theorem}
\begin{theorem}\label{fund_proj_thm}\cite{berger:geometryI}
	Let both $\mathbb{P}\left( E\right)$,   $\mathbb{P}\left( E'\right)$ two projective spaces of equal dimension  $\ge 2$ over fields $K$ and $K'$. Let $f: \mathbb{P}\left( E\right)\cong \mathbb{P}\left( E'\right)$ be a set theoretic bijection such that for any three collinear points $a, b, c \in mathbb{P}\left( E\right)$ the points $f(a), f(b), f(c) \in \mathbb{P}\left( E\right)$ are collinear then there is a semilinear map $f : E \to E'$ with $f = \underline{ \hat f}$
\end{theorem}



\begin{definition}\label{collineatioin_defn}\cite{pjective_reometty}
	A {\it collineation} of P is a bijective map from the point set and line set
	of P onto themselves that preserves incidence.
	\end{definition}
\begin{definition}\cite{pjective_reometty}
	Definition. A collineation of a projective space P(V) is called projective if it is
	induced by a linear map of V.
\end{definition}
\begin{definition}\cite{pjective_reometty}
	%Definition. 
	Let $P$ be a projective space. We say that in $P$ {\it the theorem of Desargues
		holds} if the following statement is valid. For any choice  $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $B_1$, $B_2$,  $B_3$ of points with the properties
	\begin{itemize}
		\item $A_j$, $B_j$ are collinear with a point $C$, $C\neq A_j, \neq B_j\neq C$ ($j = 1,2,3$),
		\item no three of the points $C$, $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$,  and no three of the points$C$, $B_1$, $B_2$, $B_3$
		are collinear,
		
		we have that the points
		\bean
		P_{12}\bydef A_1A_2 \cap  B_1B_2, \quad P_{23}\bydef A_2A_3 \cap  B_2B_3, \quad P_{31}\bydef A_3A_1 \cap  B_3B_1
		\eean
		lie on a common line.
	\end{itemize}
\end{definition}
\begin{theorem}\cite{pjective_reometty}
%	2.2.1 Theorem. 
Let V be a vector space of dimension d + lover a division ring
	F. Then the theorem of Desargues holds in P(V).
\end{theorem}
\begin{definition}\cite{pjective_reometty}
	Let $P$ be a projective space. We say that in $P$ {\it the theorem of Pappus
		holds} if any two intersecting lines $g$ and $h$ with $g \neq h$ satisfy the following
	condition. If  $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$ are distinct points on $g$ and 
	$B_1$, $B_2$,  $B_3$ are distinct points on $h$ all different from $g \cap h$  then the points
	\bean
	Q_{12}\bydef A_1B_2 \cap  B_1A_2, \quad Q_{23}\bydef A_2B_3 \cap  B_2A_3, \quad Q_{31}\bydef A_3B_1 \cap  B_3A_1
	\eean
	lie on a common line.
	
\end{definition}
\begin{theorem}\cite{pjective_reometty}
%	2.2.2 Theorem. 
Let V be a vector space over the division ring F. Then the theorem
	of Pappus holds in P(V) if and only if F is commutative (in other words, if
	F is afield).
\end{theorem}
\begin{theorem}\cite{pjective_reometty}
%	2.2.2 Theorem. 
Let $v$ be a vector space over the division ring $F$. Then the theorem
	of Pappus holds in $P(V)$ if and only if F is commutative (in other words, if
	F is afield).
\end{theorem}
\begin{theorem}\cite{pjective_reometty}
%2.2.3 Hessenberg's theorem. 
Let P be an arbitrary projective space. If the theorem
	of Pappus holds in P then the theorem of Desargues is also true in P.\end{theorem}
	
		\begin{empt}\label{projective_bundle_empt}\cite{bryl:loop}
		For a separable Hilbert space $E$, the {\it projective space} $\mathbb{P}$ of $E$ is a manifold 
		modeled on $E/ \C$. For $\left\{e_n\right\}$ an orthonormal basis of $E$, $E\setminus 0$ is covered by the 
		open sets $\sV_j \bydef  = \left\{\left.\sum_{n=1}^\infty x_n e^n\right| x_j \neq 0 \right\}$. $\mathbb{P}$ is covered by the open sets $\sU_j$
		The open set $\sV_j$ identifies with the subspace $\left( \C \cdot e_j\right)^\perp$  of $E$; to $v \in \left( \C \cdot e_j\right)^\perp$
		corresponds the line spanned by $v + e_j$. Again $p: E\setminus 0 \to  \mathbb{P}$ is  principal 
		$C^*$-bundle. 
	\end{empt}
	
	
\end{appendices}
